   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Hispanic
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic_culture&amp;redirect=no" title="Hispanic culture">
       Hispanic culture
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="Globe icon." data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" width="48"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          The examples and perspective in this article
          <b>
           may not represent a
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">
            worldwide view
           </a>
           of the subject
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           You may
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           , discuss the issue on the
           <a href="/wiki/Talk:Hispanic" title="Talk:Hispanic">
            talk page
           </a>
           , or
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">
            create a new article
           </a>
           , as appropriate.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (June 2015)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        <b>
         Hispanic
        </b>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         hispano
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <i>
          hispánico
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) broadly refers to the people, nations, and cultures that have a historical link to
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       . It commonly applies to countries once owned by the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       in the Americas (see
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">
        Spanish colonization of the Americas
       </a>
       ) and Asia, particularly the countries of
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">
        Hispanic America
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       . It could be argued that the term should apply to all Spanish-speaking cultures or countries, as the historical roots of the word specifically pertain to the Iberian region. It is difficult to label a nation or culture with one term, such as
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       , as the ethnicities, customs, traditions, and art forms (music, literature, dress, culture, cuisine, and others) vary greatly by country and region. The
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish language
       </a>
       and Spanish culture are the main distinctions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fhwa.dot.gov_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fhwa.dot.gov-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBA_8005_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SBA_8005-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       originally referred to the people of ancient Roman
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">
        Hispania
       </a>
       , which roughly comprised the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       , including the contemporary states of
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">
        Andorra
       </a>
       , and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territory" title="British Overseas Territory">
        British Overseas Territory
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">
        Gibraltar
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">
        Terminology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       derives from Latin
       <i>
        Hispanicus
       </i>
       ('Spanish'), the adjectival derivation of Latin (and Greek)
       <i>
        Hispania
       </i>
       ('Spain') and
       <i>
        Hispanus
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Hispanos
       </i>
       ('Spaniard'), ultimately probably of
       <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
        Celtiberian
       </a>
       origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harper-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In English the word is attested from the 16th century (and in the late 19th century in American English).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The words
       <i>
        Spain
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Spanish
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        Spaniard
       </i>
       are of the same etymology as
       <i>
        Hispanus
       </i>
       , ultimately.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harper-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:131px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="129" height="454" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg" width="129"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Stele of a family of
         <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">
          celts
         </a>
         , hispanus from
         <a href="/wiki/Gallaecia" title="Gallaecia">
          Gallaecia
         </a>
         : Apana · Ambo/lli · f(ilia) · Celtica /
         <a href="/wiki/Celtici_Supertamarici" title="Celtici Supertamarici">
          Supertam(arica)
         </a>
         · / [j] Miobri · /an(norum) · XXV · h(ic) · s(ita) · e(st) · /Apanus · fr(ater) · f(aciendum)· c(uravit)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
          <a href="#cite_note-8">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Hispanus
       </i>
       was the Latin name given to a person from Hispania during
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman rule
       </a>
       . In English, the term
       <i>
        Hispano-Roman
       </i>
       is sometimes used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pohl_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pohl-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Hispano-Romans were composed of people from many different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_peoples_of_the_Iberian_Peninsula" title="Pre-Roman peoples of the Iberian Peninsula">
        indigenous tribes
       </a>
       , in addition to
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">
        Italian
       </a>
       colonists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some famous
       <i>
        Hispani
       </i>
       (plural of
       <i>
        Hispanus
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        Hispaniensis
       </i>
       were the emperors
       <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">
        Trajan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">
        Marcus Aurelius
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">
        Hadrian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">
        Theodosius I
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Maximus" title="Magnus Maximus">
        Magnus Maximus
       </a>
       , the poets
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annaeus_Lucanus" title="Marcus Annaeus Lucanus">
        Marcus Annaeus Lucanus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Martial" title="Martial">
        Martial
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Prudentius" title="Prudentius">
        Prudentius
       </a>
       , the philosophers
       <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Elder" title="Seneca the Elder">
        Seneca the Elder
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">
        Seneca the Younger
       </a>
       , or the usurper
       <a href="/wiki/Maximus_of_Hispania" title="Maximus of Hispania">
        Maximus of Hispania
       </a>
       . A number of these men, such as Trajan, Hadrian and others, were in fact descended from Roman colonial families.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Here follows a comparison of several terms related to
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Hispania
        </i>
        was the name of the
        <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
         Iberian Peninsula
        </a>
        /Iberia from the 3rd century BC to the 8th AD, both as a
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
         Roman Empire
        </a>
        province and immediately thereafter as a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigothic_kingdom" title="Visigothic kingdom">
         Visigothic kingdom
        </a>
        , 5th–8th century.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Hispano-Roman
        </i>
        is used to refer to the culture and people of Hispania.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
         <a href="#cite_note-16">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam_Webster_Online_17-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Merriam_Webster_Online-17">
          [17]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Hispanic
        </i>
        is used to refer to modern Spain, to the Spanish language, and to the Spanish-speaking nations of the world and particularly the
        <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
         Americas
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam_Webster_Online_17-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Merriam_Webster_Online-17">
          [17]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
         <a href="#cite_note-18">
          [18]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Spanish
        </i>
        is used to refer to the people, nationality, culture, language and other things of Spain.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Spaniard
        </i>
        is used to refer to the people of Spain.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Hispania
       </i>
       was the Roman name for the whole territory of the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       . Initially, this territory was divided into two provinces:
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Citerior" title="Hispania Citerior">
        Hispania Citerior
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Ulterior" title="Hispania Ulterior">
        Hispania Ulterior
       </a>
       . In 27 B.C, Hispania Ulterior was divided into two new provinces,
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Baetica" title="Hispania Baetica">
        Hispania Baetica
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispania_Lusitania" title="Hispania Lusitania">
        Hispania Lusitania
       </a>
       , while Hispania Citerior was renamed
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Tarraconensis" title="Hispania Tarraconensis">
        Hispania Tarraconensis
       </a>
       . This division of Hispania explains the usage of the singular and plural forms (Spain, and The Spains) used to refer to the peninsula and its kingdoms in the Middle Ages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Before the marriage of Queen
       <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">
        Isabella I of Castile
       </a>
       and King
       <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">
        Ferdinand II of Aragon
       </a>
       in 1469, the four Christian kingdoms of the Iberian Peninsula—the Kingdom of
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">
        Crown of Aragon
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">
        Crown of Castile
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">
        Kingdom of Navarre
       </a>
       —were collectively called The Spains. This revival of the old Roman concept in the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       appears to have originated in
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        Provençal
       </a>
       , and was first documented at the end of the 11th century. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">
        Council of Constance
       </a>
       , the four kingdoms shared one vote.
      </p>
      <p>
       The word
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lusitanians" title="Lusitanians">
         Lusitanian
        </a>
       </i>
       , relates to Lusitania or Portugal, also in reference to the
       <a href="/wiki/Lusitanians" title="Lusitanians">
        Lusitanians
       </a>
       , possibly one of the first
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       tribes to settle in Europe. From this tribe's name had derived the name of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">
        Roman province
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">
        Lusitania
       </a>
       , and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://la.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lusitania" title="la:Lusitania">
         Lusitania
        </a>
       </i>
       remains the name of Portugal in Latin.
      </p>
      <p>
       The terms
       <i>
        Spain
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        the Spains
       </i>
       were not interchangeable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spain was a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_region" title="Geographic region">
        geographic territory
       </a>
       , home to several kingdoms (Christian and Muslim), with separate governments, laws, languages, religions, and customs, and was the historical remnant of the Hispano-Gothic unity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spain was not a political entity until much later, and when referring to the Middle Ages, one should not be confounded with the nation-state of today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term
       <i>
        The Spains
       </i>
       referred specifically to a collective of juridico-political units, first the Christian kingdoms, and then the different kingdoms ruled by the same king.
      </p>
      <p>
       With the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decretos_de_Nueva_Planta" title="Decretos de Nueva Planta">
         Decretos de Nueva Planta
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Spain" title="Philip V of Spain">
        Philip V
       </a>
       started to organize the fusion of his kingdoms that until then were ruled as distinct and independent, but this unification process lacked a formal and juridic proclamation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although colloquially and literally the expression "King of Spain" or "King of the Spains" was already widespread,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it did not refer to a unified nation-state. It was only in
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1812" title="Spanish Constitution of 1812">
        the constitution of 1812
       </a>
       that was adopted the name
       <i>
        Españas
       </i>
       (Spains) for the Spanish nation and the use of the title of "king of the Spains".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1978" title="Spanish Constitution of 1978">
        The constitution of 1876
       </a>
       adopts for the first time the name "Spain" for the Spanish nation and from then on the kings would use the title of "king of Spain".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The expansion of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       between 1492 and 1898 brought thousands of Spanish migrants to the conquered lands, who established settlements, mainly in the Americas, but also in other distant parts of the world (as in the Philippines, the lone Spanish territory in Asia), producing a number of multiracial populations. Today, the term
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       is typically applied to the varied populations of these places, including those with Spanish ancestry. The Filipinos however can be considered Hispanics because of the culture and language that Spanish left behind. Along with English and Tagalog, Spanish used to be one of the official languages in the Philippines before being removed in 1973 by the Cory Aquino government.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_in_ancient_Rome">
        Definitions in ancient Rome
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions in ancient Rome">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Latin gentile adjectives that belong to Hispania are
       <i>
        Hispanus, Hispanicus,
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Hispanienses.
       </i>
       A Hispanus is someone who is a native of Hispania with no foreign parents, while children born in Hispania of (Latin) Roman parents were Hispaniensis.
       <i>
        Hispaniensis
       </i>
       means 'connected in some way to Hispania', as in "Exercitus Hispaniensis" ('the Spanish army') or "mercatores Hispanienses" ('Spanish merchants').
       <i>
        Hispanicus
       </i>
       implies 'of' or 'belonging to' Hispania or the Hispanus or of their fashion as in "glaudius Hispanicus".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The gentile adjectives were not ethnolinguistic but derived primarily on a geographic basis, from the toponym Hispania as the people of Hispania spoke different languages, although
       <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">
        Livy
       </a>
       said they could all understand each other, not making clear if they spoke dialects of the same language or were polyglots.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first recorded use of an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthroponym" title="Anthroponym">
        anthroponym
       </a>
       derived from the toponym Hispania is attested in one of the five fragments, of
       <a href="/wiki/Ennius" title="Ennius">
        Ennius
       </a>
       in 236 B.C. who wrote "Hispane, non Romane memoretis loqui me" ("Remember that I speak like a Spaniard not a Roman") as having been said by a native of Hispania.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_in_Portugal_and_Spain">
        Definitions in Portugal and Spain
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Definitions in Portugal and Spain">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The term Hispanic signifies the cultural resonance, among other elements and characteristics, of the descendants of the people who inhabited ancient Hispania (Iberian Peninsula). It has been used throughout history for many purposes, including drawing a contrast to the Moors and differentiating explorers and settlers.
      </p>
      <p>
       Technically speaking, persons from Portugal or of Portuguese extraction are referred to as Lusitanians. In Portugal, Hispanic refers to something related to ancient Hispania, Spain or the Spanish language and culture, Portugal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Portugal and Spain do not have exactly the same definition for the term Hispanic, but they do share the etymology for the word (pt:
       <i>
        hispânico
       </i>
       , es:
       <i>
        hispánico
       </i>
       ).
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Royal_Spanish_Academy" title="The Royal Spanish Academy">
        The Royal Spanish Academy
       </a>
       (Spanish: Real Academia Española, RAE), the official royal institution responsible for regulating the Spanish language defines the terms "Hispano" and "Hispánico" (which in Spain have slightly different meanings) as:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Hispano:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Of Hispania.
       </li>
       <li>
        Belonging or relative to old Hispania.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish, as applied to a person.
       </li>
       <li>
        Of or pertaining to
        <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">
         Hispanic America
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Of or pertaining to the population of Hispanic American origin who live in the United States of America.
       </li>
       <li>
        A person of this origin who lives in the United States of America.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Hispánico:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Belonging or relative to old Hispania and the peoples which were once part of it.
       </li>
       <li>
        Belonging or relative to Spain and Spanish-speaking countries.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Note that both terms include Portugal as part of "Hispania" as Hispania is the old Roman name given to the entire Iberian peninsula and their peoples, including the Lusitanians.
      </p>
      <p>
       The common modern term to identify Portuguese and Spanish cultures under a single nomenclature is "Iberian", and the one to refer to cultures derived from both countries in the Americas is "Iberian-American". These designations can be mutually recognized by people in Portugal and Brazil, unlike "Hispanic", which is totally void of any self-identification in those countries, and quite on the opposite, serves the purpose of marking a clear distinction in relation to neighboring countries´ culture.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Spanish, the term "hispano" as in "hispanoamericano", refers to the people of Spanish origin who live in the Americas; it also refers to a relationship to Hispania or to the Spanish language. There are people in Hispanic America that are not of Spanish origin, as the original people of these areas are Amerindians.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_in_the_United_States">
        Definitions in the United States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Definitions in the United States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_United_States" title="Ethnic groups in the United States">
        Ethnic groups in the United States
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="History of Hispanic and Latino Americans">
        History of Hispanic and Latino Americans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">
        Race and ethnicity in the United States Census
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanic/Latino_naming_dispute" title="Hispanic/Latino naming dispute">
        Hispanic/Latino naming dispute
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Today, organizations in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       use the term as a broad catchall to refer to persons with a historical and cultural relationship with Spain, regardless of race and ethnicity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fhwa.dot.gov_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fhwa.dot.gov-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBA_8005_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SBA_8005-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The U.S. Census Bureau defines the
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">
        ethnonym
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
         Hispanic or Latino
        </a>
       </i>
       to refer to "a person of Cuban, Mexican, Puerto Rican, South or Central American, or other Spanish culture or origin regardless of race"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census.gov_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-census.gov-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and states that Hispanics or Latinos can be of any race, any ancestry, any ethnicity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Generically, this limits the definition of Hispanic or Latino to people from the Caribbean, Central and South America, or other Hispanic (Spanish or Portuguese) culture or origin, regardless of race. Latino can refer to males or females, while Latina refers to only females.
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of the technical distinctions involved in defining "race" vs. "ethnicity," there is confusion among the general population about the designation of Hispanic identity. Currently, the United States Census Bureau defines six race categories:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        White or Caucasian
       </li>
       <li>
        Black or African American
       </li>
       <li>
        American Indian or Alaska Native
       </li>
       <li>
        Asian
       </li>
       <li>
        Native Hawaiian or Other Pacific Islander
       </li>
       <li>
        Some Other Race
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       According to census reports, of the above races the largest number of Hispanic or Latinos are of the White race, the second largest number come from the Native American/American Indian race who are the indigenous people of the Americas. The inhabitants of Easter Island are Pacific Islanders and since the island belongs to Chile they are theoretically Hispanic or Latinos. Because Hispanic roots are considered aligned with a European ancestry (Spain/Portugal), Hispanic/Latino ancestry is defined solely as an
       <i>
        ethnic
       </i>
       designation (similar to being Norse or Germanic). Therefore, a person of Hispanic descent is typically defined using both race and ethnicity as an identifier—i.e., Black-Hispanic, White-Hispanic, Asian-Hispanic, Amerindian-Hispanic or "other race" Hispanic.
      </p>
      <p>
       A 1997 notice by the U.S.
       <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Office of Management and Budget">
        Office of Management and Budget
       </a>
       defined
       <i>
        Hispanic or Latino
       </i>
       persons as being "persons who trace their origin or descent to Mexico, Puerto Rico, Cuba, Central and South America, and other Spanish cultures."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omb_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-omb-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">
        United States Census
       </a>
       uses the
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">
        ethnonym
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
         Hispanic or Latino
        </a>
       </i>
       to refer to "a person of Cuban, Mexican, Puerto Rican, South or Central American, or other Hispanic culture or origin regardless of race."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census.gov_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-census.gov-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">
        2010 Census
       </a>
       asked if the person was "Spanish/Hispanic/Latino". The
       <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">
        United States Census
       </a>
       uses the
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">
        ethnonym
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
         Hispanic or Latino
        </a>
       </i>
       to refer to "a person of Cuban, Mexican, Puerto Rican, South or Central American, or other Spanish culture or origin regardless of race."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census.gov_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-census.gov-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Census Bureau also explains that "[o]rigin can be viewed as the heritage, nationality group, lineage, or country of birth of the person or the person's ancestors before their arrival in the United States. People who identify their origin as Hispanic, Latino, or Spanish may be of any race."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">
        U.S. Department of Transportation
       </a>
       defines
       <i>
        Hispanic
       </i>
       as, "persons of Mexican, Puerto Rican, Cuban, Dominican, Central or South American, or other Spanish or Portuguese culture or origin, regardless of race."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fhwa.dot.gov_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-fhwa.dot.gov-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This definition has been adopted by the
       <a href="/wiki/Small_Business_Administration" title="Small Business Administration">
        Small Business Administration
       </a>
       as well as by many federal, state, and municipal agencies for the purposes of awarding government contracts to minority owned businesses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBA_8005_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-SBA_8005-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Hispanic_Caucus" title="Congressional Hispanic Caucus">
        Congressional Hispanic Caucus
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Hispanic_Conference" title="Congressional Hispanic Conference">
        Congressional Hispanic Conference
       </a>
       include representatives of Spanish and Portuguese, Puerto Rican and Mexican descent. The
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_Society_of_America" title="Hispanic Society of America">
        Hispanic Society of America
       </a>
       is dedicated to the study of the arts and cultures of
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin America
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Hispanic Association of Colleges and Universities, proclaimed champions of Hispanic success in higher education, is committed to Hispanic educational success in the U.S., Puerto Rico, Ibero-America, Spain and Portugal.
      </p>
      <p>
       The U.S.
       <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">
        Equal Employment Opportunity Commission
       </a>
       encourages any individual who believes that he or she is Hispanic to self-identify as Hispanic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Labor" title="United States Department of Labor">
        United States Department of Labor
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Federal_Contract_Compliance_Programs" title="Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs">
        Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs
       </a>
       encourages the same self-identification. As a result, any individual who traces his or her origins to part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">
        Portuguese Empire
       </a>
       may self-identify as Hispanic, because an employer may not override an individual's self-identification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/1970_United_States_Census" title="1970 United States Census">
        1970 Census
       </a>
       was the first time that a "Hispanic" identifier was used and data collected with the question. The definition of "Hispanic" has been modified in each successive census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a recent study, most Spanish-speakers of Spanish or Hispanic American descent do not prefer the term "Hispanic" or "Latino" when it comes to describing their identity. Instead, they prefer to be identified by their country of origin. When asked if they have a preference for either being identified as "Hispanic" or "Latino," the Pew study finds that "half (51%) say they have no preference for either term."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A majority (51%) say they most often identify themselves by their family’s country of origin, while 24% say they prefer a pan-ethnic label such as Hispanic or Latino. Among those 24% who have a preference for a pan-ethnic label, "'Hispanic' is preferred over 'Latino' by more than a two-to-one margin—33% versus 14%." Twenty-one percent prefer to be referred to simply as "Americans."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hispanicization">
        Hispanicization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hispanicization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Hispanicization is the process by which a place or a person absorbs characteristics of Hispanic society and culture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Modern hispanization of a place, namely in the United States, might be illustrated by Spanish-language media and businesses. Hispanization of a person might be illustrated by speaking Spanish, making and eating Hispanic American food, listening to Spanish language music or participating in Hispanic festivals and holidays - Hispanization of those outside the Hispanic community as opposed to
       <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">
        assimilation
       </a>
       of Hispanics into theirs.
      </p>
      <p>
       One reason that some people believe the assimilation of Hispanics in the U.S. is not comparable to that of other cultural groups is that
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
        Hispanic and Latino Americans
       </a>
       have been living in parts of North America for centuries, in many cases well before the English-speaking culture became dominant. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
        California
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
        Texas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">
        Colorado
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
        New Mexico
       </a>
       (1598),
       <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">
        Arizona
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">
        Nevada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
        Florida
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">
        Puerto Rico
       </a>
       have been home to Spanish-speaking peoples since the 16th century, long before the U.S. existed. (The language of the Native Americans existed before this, until the invasion and forced assimilation by the Spanish.) These and other Spanish-speaking territories were part of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">
        Viceroyalty of New Spain
       </a>
       , and later
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       (with the exception of Florida and Puerto Rico), before these regions joined or were taken over by the United States in 1848. Some cities in the U.S. were founded by Spanish settlers as early as the 16th century, prior to the creation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">
        Thirteen Colonies
       </a>
       . For example, San Miguel de Gualdape,
       <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">
        Pensacola
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine,_Florida" title="St. Augustine, Florida">
        St. Augustine, Florida
       </a>
       were founded in 1526, 1559 and 1565 respectively.
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">
        Santa Fe, New Mexico
       </a>
       was founded in 1604, and
       <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">
        Albuquerque
       </a>
       was established in 1660.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Paso" title="El Paso">
        El Paso
       </a>
       was founded in 1659,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">
        San Antonio
       </a>
       in 1691,
       <a href="/wiki/Laredo,_Texas" title="Laredo, Texas">
        Laredo, Texas
       </a>
       in 1755,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">
        San Diego
       </a>
       in 1769,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">
        San Francisco
       </a>
       in 1776,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">
        San Jose, California
       </a>
       in 1777,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Iberia,_Louisiana" title="New Iberia, Louisiana">
        New Iberia, Louisiana
       </a>
       in 1779, and
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </a>
       in 1781. Therefore, in many parts of the U.S., the Hispanic cultural legacy predates English/British influence. For this reason, many generations have largely maintained their cultural traditions and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish language
       </a>
       well before the United States was created. However, Spanish-speaking persons in many Hispanic areas in the U.S. amounted to only a few thousand people when they became part of the United States; a large majority of current Hispanic residents are descended from Hispanics who entered the United States in the mid-to-late 20th and early 21st centuries.
      </p>
      <p>
       Language retention is a common index to assimilation; according to the 2000 census, about 75 percent of all Hispanics spoke Spanish in the home. Spanish language retention rates vary geographically; parts of Texas and New Mexico have language retention rates over 90 percent, whereas in parts of Colorado and California, retention rates are lower than 30 percent. The degree of retention of Spanish as the native language is based on recent arrival from countries where Spanish is spoken. As is true of other immigrants, those who were born in other countries still speak their native language. Later generations are increasingly less likely to speak the language spoken in the country of their ancestors, as is true of other immigrant groups.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish-speaking_countries_and_regions">
        Spanish-speaking countries and regions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hispanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spanish-speaking countries and regions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table align="right" class="wikitable">
       <tr style="background:#FFFF00;" valign="top">
        <td align="center">
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Hispanophone" title="Hispanophone">
           Spanish-speaking countries
          </a>
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>

        <td>
         <div class="center">

           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg" title="Spanish-Speaking World">
            <img alt="Spanish-Speaking World" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg/250px-Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg/375px-Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg/500px-Countries_with_Spanish_as_an_official_language.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>

           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #000080; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            Spanish identified as co-official language
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanophone" title="Hispanophone">
        Hispanophone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">
        Hispanic America
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_where_Spanish_is_an_official_language" title="List of countries where Spanish is an official language">
        List of countries where Spanish is an official language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Today, Spanish is among the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">
        most commonly spoken first languages
       </a>
       of the world. During the period of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       from 1492 and 1898, many people migrated from Spain to the conquered lands. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaniard" title="Spaniard">
        Spaniards
       </a>
       brought with them the Castilian language and culture, and in this
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">
        process that lasted several centuries
       </a>
       , created a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_empire" title="Global empire">
        global empire
       </a>
       with a diverse population.
      </p>
      <p>
       Culturally, Spaniards (those living in Spain) are typically European, but they also have small traces of many peoples from the rest of Europe, such as for example, old Germania, Scandinavia, France, the Mediterranean, the Near East and northern Africa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

         English 79.4%, Spanish 12.8%, other Indo-European 3.7%, Asian and Pacific Islander languages 3.0%, other 0.9% (2010 census) (Hawaiian is an official language in the state of Hawaii).
         <p>
          (
          <i>
           <b>
            Note:
           </b>
           The U.S. is a predominantly English-speaking country. As is true of many immigrant families, the immigrants often speak Spanish and some English, while their children are fluent English speakers because they were born and educated in the U.S. Some retain their Spanish language as is true of other immigrant families. The recent influx of large numbers of immigrants from Spanish-speaking countries into the U.S. has meant that the number of Spanish-speaking U.S. residents has increased, but the children speaking English as is true of the historic U.S. immigrant experience, continues. Migration from Hispanic countries has increased the Spanish-speaking population in the United States. Of those who speak Spanish in the United States, three quarters speak English well or very well.
          </i>
         </p>

            </b>
            a separate listing for
            <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
             Hispanics
            </a>
            is not included because the U.S. Census Bureau considers Hispanic to mean a person of Hispanic American descent (including persons of Cuban, Mexican, or Puerto Rican origin) and of Spanish descent living in the U.S. who may be of any race or ethnic group (white, black, Asian, etc.); about 15–16% of the total U.S. population is Hispanic, not including estimates about
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_alien" title="Legal alien">
             alien residents
            </a>
           </i>
           )
          </small>
          .
         </p>
        </td>

         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mestizo_Colombian" title="Mestizo Colombian">

         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Colombian" title="White Colombian">

         </a>

          Black
         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Zambo" title="Zambo">
          Zambo
         </a>
         ),
         <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Colombia" title="Indigenous peoples in Colombia">
          Amerindian
         </a>
         3.4%,
         <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">
          Roma
         </a>
         0.01%, among other ethnic groups.
         <br/>
         <small>
          <i>
           (See:
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colombian_people" title="Colombian people">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Spanish-based_creole_languages" title="Spanish-based creole languages">

         </a>
         is spoken in the Philippines by 600,000 people.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phil_84-0">

           [84]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Spanish" title="Philippine Spanish">
          Philippine Spanish
         </a>
         is natively spoken by 5,000 people but second- and third-language speakers range from 500,000 to 2,500,000.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
          <a href="#cite_note-85">
           [85]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">

          </a>
         </sup>

          </a>
         </sup>

          Hispanic influences
         </a>
         have impacted several native languages, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
          Tagalog
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">

         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">
          Ilocano
         </a>
         . Many aspects of Filipino culture including cuisine, traditional dances, music, festivals, religion, architecture, traditional costumes and crafts exhibit Hispanic origin and influences.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phil_84-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-phil-84">
           [84]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Filipino" title="Spanish Filipino">
          Spanish Filipino
         </a>
         . Various ethnolinguistic groups particularly with some Hispanic heritage that forms up the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filipino_people" title="Filipino people">

         (

         Former Spanish territories in Asia-Pacific no longer recognize Spanish as an official language. The predominant languages used in Guam are English, Chamorro and Filipino. Also, in Guam – a U.S. territory – and the Northern Mariana Islands, a commonwealth in political union with the U.S., a
         <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">
          Malayo-Polynesian language
         </a>
         called
         <a href="/wiki/Chamorro_language" title="Chamorro language">
          Chamorro
         </a>
         is spoken, with numerous loanwords with Spanish etymological origins. However it is not a Spanish creole language.

         </a>
